  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_57010f06-5133-4488-86bb-425c2119d4cf'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };

      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer7686884221786540173">While Eisenstein documented the forces of totalitarianism and Stalinism upon the faces of the Russian peoples, DeLillo offers a stunning, at times overwhelming, document of the twin forces of the Cold War and American culture, compelling that "swerve from evenness" in which he finds events and people both wondrous and horrifying.<br/><br/><i>Underworld</i> opens with a breathlessly gracefu</span>
  <span id="freeText7686884221786540173" style="display:none">While Eisenstein documented the forces of totalitarianism and Stalinism upon the faces of the Russian peoples, DeLillo offers a stunning, at times overwhelming, document of the twin forces of the Cold War and American culture, compelling that "swerve from evenness" in which he finds events and people both wondrous and horrifying.<br /><br /><i>Underworld</i> opens with a breathlessly graceful prologue set during the final game of the Giants-Dodgers pennant race in 1951. Written in what DeLillo calls "super-omniscience" the sentences sweep from young Cotter Martin as he jumps the gate to the press box, soars over the radio waves, runs out to the diamond, slides in on a fast ball, pops into the stands where J. Edgar Hoover is sitting with a drunken Jackie Gleason and a splenetic Frank Sinatra, and learns of the Soviet Union's second detonation of a nuclear bomb. It's an absolutely thrilling literary moment. When Bobby Thomson hits Branca's pitch into the outstretched hand of Cotter—the "shot heard around the world"—and Jackie Gleason pukes on Sinatra's shoes, the events of the next few decades are set in motion, all threaded together by the baseball as it passes from hand to hand.<br /><br />"It's all falling indelibly into the past," writes DeLillo, a past that he carefully recalls and reconstructs with acute grace. Jump from Giants Stadium to the Nevada desert in 1992, where Nick Shay, who now owns the baseball, reunites with the artist Kara Sax. They had been brief and unlikely lovers 40 years before, and it is largely through the events, spinoffs, and coincidental encounters of their pasts that DeLillo filters the Cold War experience. He believes that "global events may alter how we live in the smallest ways," and as the book steps back in time to 1951, over the following 800-odd pages, we see just how those events alter lives. This reverse narrative allows the author to strip away the detritus of history and pop culture until we get to the story's pure elements: the bomb, the baseball, and the Bronx. In an epilogue as breathless and stunning as the prologue, DeLillo fast-forwards to a near future in which ruthless capitalism, the Internet, and a new, hushed faith have replaced the Cold War's blend of dread and euphoria.<br /><br />Through fragments and interlaced stories—including those of highway killers, artists, celebrities, conspiracists, gangsters, nuns, and sundry others—DeLillo creates a fragile web of connected experience, a communal Zeitgeist that encompasses the messy whole of five decades of American life, wonderfully distilled.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7686884221786540173" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer78526452" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8497379725412248638">seriously, why does everyone suck this book's dick so much? <br/><br/>this book was recommended to me by an ex (who also recommended <i>zuleika dobson</i> and <i>the joke</i>, so he had a good track record until then) who knew how much i liked <i>infinite jest</i> so he thought i would like this one. and if i only liked infinite jest because it was a long book written by a white male, then i suppose i would have liked this book. but i didn't, so it must be something else i'm drawn to in the wallace.<br/><br/>i remember i was reading th</span>
  <span id="freeText8497379725412248638" style="display:none">seriously, why does everyone suck this book's dick so much? <br><br>this book was recommended to me by an ex (who also recommended <i>zuleika dobson</i> and <i>the joke</i>, so he had a good track record until then) who knew how much i liked <i>infinite jest</i> so he thought i would like this one. and if i only liked infinite jest because it was a long book written by a white male, then i suppose i would have liked this book. but i didn't, so it must be something else i'm drawn to in the wallace.<br><br>i remember i was reading this at the airport where i was going to meet him, like a dutiful girlfriend, and just having my jaw drop at the first part. not because it was soooo goooood like everyone here seems to think. am i really the only one who felt embarrassed by the whole <i>life</i> magazine thing? i remember looking around after i read that part to see if someone was playing a trick on me. when he got off the plane, i just sat there, shaking my head at him sadly. it was the beginning of the end.<br><br>look - i really liked <i>white noise</i>, but this i just felt to be a bloated, wooden, oddly-phrased book whose language didn't charm me, but made me unhappy. and then he goes and publishes the first bit as a separate book? who does that?? sorry, delillo - its not terrible, so it gets no 2 stars, but i barely cared about anything in this book, and it ruined a relationship. if i die alone, it's your fault.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1528725630i/25720315.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><a href="http://bloggycomelately.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">come to my blog!</a><br></span>

<div id="review_858320801" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/858320801" />
    <a title="Violet wells" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4196681-violet-wells"><img alt="Violet wells" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442260743p2/4196681.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/858320801?book_show_action=true">Feb 18, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Violet wells" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Violet wells" href="/user/show/4196681-violet-wells">Violet wells</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4196681-violet-wells?shelf=new-york">new-york</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4196681-violet-wells?shelf=faves">faves</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4196681-violet-wells?shelf=contemporary-american-fiction">contemporary-american-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4196681-violet-wells?shelf=pulitzer">pulitzer</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer858320801" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1287855612874181852">I love reading James Wood on the novel. For me he’s up there with Virginia Woolf as a critic who genuinely enriches the experience of reading the novel. Even though he often denigrates authors I love. Don Delillo for example. Underworld for Wood was gratuitously obsessed with paranoia as if this was a concern peculiar to only Delillo. But one could say paranoia was a state of mind invented by America. Did it even exist in the 19th century? The Cold War saw the invention of paranoia as a mass med</span>
  <span id="freeText1287855612874181852" style="display:none">I love reading James Wood on the novel. For me he’s up there with Virginia Woolf as a critic who genuinely enriches the experience of reading the novel. Even though he often denigrates authors I love. Don Delillo for example. Underworld for Wood was gratuitously obsessed with paranoia as if this was a concern peculiar to only Delillo. But one could say paranoia was a state of mind invented by America. Did it even exist in the 19th century? The Cold War saw the invention of paranoia as a mass media tool for manipulating public opinion. Delillo’s fascination with it was not only entirely legitimate but incredibly eye-opening in tracing the changing psyche of post 1950 America. I don’t have Wood’s book with me here but to my recollection he wrote brilliantly about Underworld without getting it.<br /><br />Underworld doesn’t have much in the way of plot. It’s like the literary equivalent of a musician jamming on a theme. As if DeLillo has submitted wholly to the tides of inspiration and allowed himself to be taken wherever they lead him. It reminded me, in form, of a web page full of hyperlinks. DeLillo is fascinated by the ghost paths of connections and the panoramic grids they form; the secret lives of objects and the far reaching stories they tell.<br /><br />He wanted an object that would provide a surreptitious link to fifty years of American history and chose the baseball that won the 1951 World Series, during which – here’s one of the hyperlinks - the Russians tested their first atomic bomb. The ball is initially pocketed by a young black kid who has jumped the turnstile without paying. From the game itself, seen through the eyes of various celebrities, we enter the life of an impoverished black family in Harlem. The first intimately observed narrative begins. <br /><br />There’s so much in this novel it’s inevitable some “storylines” will appeal more than others. Ultimately, it’s the clairvoyant power and beautiful urban lyricism of the prose which makes this a masterpiece in my eyes. DeLillo is like a soothsayer of the technological consumerist age. (“Bemoan technology all you want. It expands your self-esteem and connects you in your well-pressed suit to the things that slip through the world otherwise unperceived.”) He takes you behind the glossy surfaces of contemporary life, excavates for deeper meaning in the newsreel footage. The novel’s central character is employed by the waste industry which perhaps epitomises perfectly the buried volatile poisoning underworld of our culture.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1287855612874181852" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_7022325" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/7022325" />
    <a title="Paul Bryant" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant"><img alt="Paul Bryant" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1224113172p2/416390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/7022325?book_show_action=true">Sep 30, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Bryant" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Bryant" href="/user/show/416390-paul-bryant">Paul Bryant</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/416390-paul-bryant?shelf=novels">novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer7022325" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12756212894543642101">THE PILGRIM 'S HEART IS LIGHT AT THE COMMENCEMENT OF HIS JOURNEY<br/><br/>So I will strap on my backpack and don sturdy walking boots, an oxygen tank might be useful, and a supply of plasters and animal pelts - and then I will begin to scale the North Face of Modern American Literature. Let's see how far I get before I fall off one of its jagged cliffs or collapse choking with one of Mr DeLillo's sentences wrapped around my neck.<br/><br/>BUT DISCOURAGEMENTS ARISE UNBIDDEN<br/><br/>Update - Not even on page 100 and I have a </span>
  <span id="freeText12756212894543642101" style="display:none">THE PILGRIM 'S HEART IS LIGHT AT THE COMMENCEMENT OF HIS JOURNEY<br /><br />So I will strap on my backpack and don sturdy walking boots, an oxygen tank might be useful, and a supply of plasters and animal pelts - and then I will begin to scale the North Face of Modern American Literature. Let's see how far I get before I fall off one of its jagged cliffs or collapse choking with one of Mr DeLillo's sentences wrapped around my neck.<br /><br />BUT DISCOURAGEMENTS ARISE UNBIDDEN<br /><br />Update - Not even on page 100 and I have a sinking feeling. It's DeLillo's style. It's so very...er...ornate. No noun escapes without an adjective pinned to it, some of which are very odd - consider these from pages 63 to 65:<br /><br />"... the little splat of human speech" [huh?:]<br />"A bled-white sky with ticky breezes" [ticky? like a clock?:]<br />"...a horseman with scabbarded rifle or a lone cameleer hunched in muslin on his dumb-headed beast"<br />"...the studded vegetation" [with what?:]<br />"...a clear night with swirled stars" [swirled?:]<br /><br />Also this -<br /><br />"There is something about old times that's satisfied by spontaneity. The quicker you decide, the more fully you discharge the debt to memory." Okay, what debt would that be? What's the logic here? Is this something our Don believes or is this something he wants us to believe this particular character believes? If so, why? Who has the time to figure out what it means anyway? Especially when there's another 762 ticky swirled studded scabbarded pages to go....<br /><br />This isn't going so well.<br /><br />DESPAIR INGULFS HIS HEART AND HE HEARS VOICES<br /><br />And finally :<br /><br />Once more despondent and unenthused, I zipped around the goodread reviews and found remarks such as<br /><br />"... oh, god... this, this... painful verbal bukakefest is literally 800 pages of DeLillo jacking off at his computer over how deep and verbose he is. i wanted to punch him in the face and shake him, shouting, "JUST GET TO THE FUCKING PLOT, YOU SELF-LOVING PIECE OF SHIT." (from Ethan)<br /><br />and<br /><br />"I'll be honest and say that I don't remember much about this book other than an awful lot of baseball. This is partially because there is a lot of baseball in it" (from Chelsea)<br /><br />and<br /><br />"Ultimately, I don't think DeLillo knew what his story was about and tried to compenstate by adding more and more pages. Critics, never wanting to be the one who doesn't "get it", fawned and fellated the book, doing no favors to either the author or readers who mistakenly wade into this dank swamp and wonder why they're so dumb for not seeing the brilliance. And then they run back to James Patterson or Nicholas Sparks or some shit like that and we're all a little poorer in the end. ." (from Joseph)<br /><br />and finally this from an online lit journal:<br /><br />"Potentially intriguing plots which feature strongly in the earlier parts of the book - an intriguing serial killer subplot, the stories of each person who possesses the winning baseball - are abandoned halfway through the book in favour of overlong childhood memories or the inane ponderings of a performance artist; other stories are neglected for over 400 pages before reappearing at the end of the novel, causing an unwelcome jolt as the reader tries to remember the pertinent details."<br /><br />THE PILGRIM CASTS THE DEVIL FROM HIM<br /><br />I groaned and decided to place this great tome gently onto my "Abandoned Halfway And Will Never Finish Unless Some Very Unlikely DeLillo Fans Take My Family Hostage" shelf.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12756212894543642101" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_4124891" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/4124891" />
    <a title="Ethan Fixell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/240989-ethan-fixell"><img alt="Ethan Fixell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1430171457p2/240989.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/4124891?book_show_action=true">Aug 05, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ethan Fixell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ethan Fixell" href="/user/show/240989-ethan-fixell">Ethan Fixell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/436005.Underworld">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">masochists</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer4124891" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5241994367869746851">i've only put down three books in my entire life. <br/><br/>the first was Ayn Rand's "Atlas Shrugged," which i absolutely loved but got terribly sick of after about 700 pages of the same goddamn philosophy being crammed down my throat. (which sounds like its awful, but i really did adore those first two thirds).<br/><br/>the second was a speed reading book. it wasn't a very quick read, and i got bored.<br/><br/>the third is now Don DeLillo's Underworld, supposedly one of the greatest masterpieces of 20th century literature.<br/><br/>i</span>
  <span id="freeText5241994367869746851" style="display:none">i've only put down three books in my entire life. <br /><br />the first was Ayn Rand's "Atlas Shrugged," which i absolutely loved but got terribly sick of after about 700 pages of the same goddamn philosophy being crammed down my throat. (which sounds like its awful, but i really did adore those first two thirds).<br /><br />the second was a speed reading book. it wasn't a very quick read, and i got bored.<br /><br />the third is now Don DeLillo's Underworld, supposedly one of the greatest masterpieces of 20th century literature.<br /><br />i have no shame in saying that i stopped reading this bullshit after 550 pages. because as "brilliant" as DeLillo may be (and granted, he does have a more-than-firm grasp on the english language and on the power of dialogue), he is absolutely, hands down, one of the most long winded, convoluted writers i have ever read. <br /><br />i've done "White Noise," and got through it without too much discomfort, but was ultimately let down by the end. and i mean that in both senses of the phrase--the ending sucked, and i was considerably less interested by the time the book ended than when i started. nevertheless, i'd still recommend it for certain redeeming qualities.<br /><br />but this one... oh, god... this, this... painful verbal bukakefest is literally 800 pages of DeLillo jacking off at his computer over how deep and verbose he is. i wanted to punch him in the face and shake him, shouting, "JUST GET TO THE FUCKING PLOT, YOU SELF-LOVING PIECE OF SHIT."<br /><br />there's nothing wrong with elegant, poetic writing, even in novel form. but without a fucking interesting narrative? <br /><br />last time i checked, a novel is defined as:<br />1.	a fictitious prose narrative of considerable length and complexity, portraying characters and usually presenting a sequential organization of action and scenes.<br /><br />yeah, i get it: he's such a fucking genius because of the way he weaves esoteric and seemingly unrelated themes throughout the lives of dozens of characters within a bevvy of settings and a nonlinear timeframe.<br /><br />but WHO FUCKING CARES?<br /><br />if its boring and the characters suck, who really fucking CARES? i don't want to read that shit. i could crack open my 10th grade chemistry textbook for that.<br /><br />i came here to read a STORY, Don. it's a shame you couldn't help.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5241994367869746851" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

What follows is discursive and ephemeral like some new kind of music. Reading it was li</span>
  <span id="freeText12607180590643633196" style="display:none">People married, were born, and died in the time it took me to read this book. A kid sitting next to me on a plane commented "that's the fattest book I've ever seen. What's it about?" I told him "I have no idea--I'm only 580 pages into it." Having finished I still don't know what it was about but reading it was an extraordinary experience. The novella that introduces the book is perfect and complete in itself. What follows is discursive and ephemeral like some new kind of music. Reading it was like learning how to listen.</span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/74596131" />
    <a title="Manny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1713956-manny"><img alt="Manny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442491692p2/1713956.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/74596131?book_show_action=true">Oct 15, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Manny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Manny" href="/user/show/1713956-manny">Manny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer74596131" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6264988184599839674">I'm surprised to see how many people here had the exact same reaction I did. They start reading, they find a few bits that seem quite gripping and well-written, they lose momentum, and they stop. Some hypotheses:<br/><br/>- None of us are smart enough to get the point.<br/><br/>- There is a clear point, but you have to reach the end to discover what it is, and we didn't have the requisite fortitude. (Also, it must be like <i>The Mousetrap</i>: readers who find out are sworn not to reveal it).<br/><br/>- The point is that life feels</span>
  <span id="freeText6264988184599839674" style="display:none">I'm surprised to see how many people here had the exact same reaction I did. They start reading, they find a few bits that seem quite gripping and well-written, they lose momentum, and they stop. Some hypotheses:<br /><br />- None of us are smart enough to get the point.<br /><br />- There is a clear point, but you have to reach the end to discover what it is, and we didn't have the requisite fortitude. (Also, it must be like <i>The Mousetrap</i>: readers who find out are sworn not to reveal it).<br /><br />- The point is that life feels this way if you're a certain kind of person, i.e. interesting in places but ultimately pretty meaningless.<br /><br />- The book just isn't very good.<br /><br />Now that I write it down, I do feel vaguely interested in discovering which of the above guesses is closest to the truth. But not interested enough to open it again.<br /><br />When I try to imagine <i>Untitled</i>, the spectacularly unsuccessful novel that Richard writes in Martin Amis's <i>The Information</i>, I must admit that the first thing I think of is <i>Underworld</i>. At least DeLillo's book doesn't cause nose-bleeds, sinus headaches or inexplicable drowsiness. Okay, maybe the last one.<br />__________________________________________<br /><br />I note with interest that Karl Ove Knausgård is another member of the club. A passage from near the end of <i>Min kamp 2</i> (he has just visited a bookstore and made some purchases):<blockquote>DeLillo-romanen angret jeg på i det samme jeg kom ut, for selv om jeg en gang hade hadde vært fan av ham, særlig romanene <i>The Names</i> og <i>White Noise</i>, hadde jeg ikke klart å lese mer en halve av <i>Underworld</i>, og siden neste bok hadde vært forferderlig, var det åpenbart at han var på hell.<br /><br />My translation:<br /><br />I regretted the DeLillo novel the moment I came out, since even if I once had been a fan, particularly of the novels <i>The Names</i> and <i>White Noise</i>, I hadn't been able to read more than half of <i>Underworld</i>, and considering that the next book had been terrible, it was clear he was on the way down.</blockquote><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6264988184599839674" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1678734458" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1678734458" />
    <a title="Perry" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6354112-perry"><img alt="Perry" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588528919p2/6354112.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1678734458?book_show_action=true">Oct 19, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Perry" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Perry" href="/user/show/6354112-perry">Perry</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1678734458" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12306744568382537630"><b>Elegy for Left Hand Alone</b><br/><i>Title of Part 2</i> <br/>[*4.5 stars*] [footnote added on 10/21]<br/><br/>I just read what to me is likely the most far-reaching American novel in terms of its scope, spanning the 1950s through the 1990s and covering a wide range of American topics, from baseball to solid waste disposal, U.S. nuclear weapons and the Soviet atomic weapons program (i.e., nuclear proliferation), guns, graffiti, the U.S. Roman Catholic Church, the Cuban Missile Crisis, drug addiction, AIDS, marital infidelity,</span>
  <span id="freeText12306744568382537630" style="display:none"><b>Elegy for Left Hand Alone</b><br><i>Title of Part 2</i> <br>[*4.5 stars*] [footnote added on 10/21]<br><br>I just read what to me is likely the most far-reaching American novel in terms of its scope, spanning the 1950s through the 1990s and covering a wide range of American topics, from baseball to solid waste disposal, U.S. nuclear weapons and the Soviet atomic weapons program (i.e., nuclear proliferation), guns, graffiti, the U.S. Roman Catholic Church, the Cuban Missile Crisis, drug addiction, AIDS, marital infidelity, and pulling in a litany of American legends like Lenny Bruce, J. Edgar Hoover and Frank Sinatra.<br><br>The novel opens with a lengthy prologue (perhaps the longest I've read) set primarily on October 3, 1951 at the New York Giants' home field, the Upper Manhattan Polo Grounds in a renowned game with the Brooklyn Dodgers to decide the National League pennant winner to play in the World Series. In the bottom of the 9th inning, the Dodgers were up 4-2, and two men were on base when a player named Bobby Thomson stepped up to the plate and hit a 3-run walk-off (game ending) homerun to give the Giants the win 5-4. <br><br>The homer has gained a sort of mythical status among baseball fans (such as myself), known as "The Shot Heard 'Round the World." The whereabouts of that baseball is still unknown in real life. But DeLillo creates a young fellow who skipped school and sneaked into the game and a scenario in which this student named Cotter Martin is befriended by an older man and we follow their conversation through parts of the game. The homer is initially caught by the older guy and Cotter wrests the ball away from him and runs home. Yet his father, a drunk, takes the ball out of his room as Cotter sleeps and sells it for $32.45.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1477032813i/20908410.jpg" width="444" height="346" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><i>Front page of New York Times on October 4, 1951</i><br><br>The remainder of the book follows a very nonlinear narrative, mostly about a guy named Nick Shay who is an executive VP at a waste disposal company. Shay grew up in Brooklyn. And his life is slowly unfolded, where we learn that he shot a guy when he was a juvenile, around the same time as he was having an affair with a 30-something married woman. DeLillo writes as if he's a bit repressed when it comes to carnal relations. Nick messes around on his wife and his best friend/co-worker is having an affair with Nick's wife.<br><br>While Nick is the novel's centerpiece, DeLillo blends in a number of themes (some of which are listed above) and integrates a mosaic of memorable luminaries, the primary two being Hoover and Bruce. Several times, he goes to bits of Bruce's routines in the early 1960s slamming and riffing on the Cuban Missile crisis and nuclear proliferation. Part of Lenny Bruce's routine discussing a guy (generally speaking) on a date : <blockquote>"you're thinking all the universal things men have always thought about and said to each other, get in her pants? did you get in? did you get some? did you make it? how far'd you get? how far'd she go? is she an easy lay? is she a good hump? is she a piece? did you get a piece? it's like the language of yard goods, piece goods, you can make her, she can be made, it's like a garment factory, ... he's a makeout artist, she's a piece, ....** </blockquote> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1477033171i/20908438._SX540_.jpg" width="300" height="210" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>The Underworld Hoover likes sneaking little peaks at his right-hand man showering and changing.<br><br>The titles of most of the parts are quite memorable, including the DuPont ad slogan, "Better Things for Better Living Through Chemistry," and the song titles, "Long Tall Sally," by Little Richard, and an infamous Rolling Stones song, not released on any album, called "Cocksucker Blues." The title of the prologue was "The Triumph of Death," a 16th Century oil painting by Dutch artist Pieter Brugel the Elder.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1477032813i/20908411.jpg" width="444" height="333" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1477032813i/20908412._SX540_.jpg" width="444" height="316" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><i>"The Triumph of Death," which fascinated Hoover in the novel</i><br><br><br>I don't know if I subscribe to this being "The Great American Novel," as a couple of critics have claimed, yet I don't think it's too far off, with such a clever and cunning layout to the book, an intelligent treatment of a number of American themes, drawing in a number of known characters, and its imaginative breadth. My only complaints were that the nonlinear narrative is a little hard to follow and the dialogue of what seems to be a conversation in which two people are talking but it sure doesn't seem like they're conversing with each other, which gets on my nerves.<br><br>_________________________<br><br>**I'll admit I heard this type of banter in college, and will further plead no contest to having said at least one of these things to close friends when I was <b>fourteen</b> and didn't even know what a piece was [seriously, but realize that I was 14 in 1979]. Yet, I can swear that in my numerous years in grade school locker rooms or in a group of beer-fueled college buddies swapping juvenile tales, I never once heard a guy say that he grabbed a girl by her crotch or her breast. Never. At 14, in 1979, I knew better than to ever touch a girl there or there. <br><br>Nonetheless, we have a man one step away from being elected POTUS who thought he was entitled to do that, in his late 50s, in the aughts. Or, at the least, joked about doing that? Wow. SMH. Where are the social conservatives, those who argue for censorship in schools to protect kids from smut? Shouldn't they be raising a ruckus? No, they are too busy trying to sell bullshit from Trump about how <s>9</s> <i>
  <b>12</b>
</i> women, each and every one of them, are lying and how SNL is part of a grand conspiracy to steal the election from a brazen, irreligious New Yorker. Hypocrisy? A sign that the apocalypse is upon us? </span>
  <a data-text-id="12306744568382537630" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review1678734458">
  <td colspan="2">

<div id="review_1699976053" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1699976053" />
    <a title="Michael Finocchiaro" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/48483884-michael-finocchiaro"><img alt="Michael Finocchiaro" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1576692048p2/48483884.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1699976053?book_show_action=true">Jul 16, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780330524971" href="/book/show/10418236-underworld">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48483884-michael-finocchiaro?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48483884-michael-finocchiaro?shelf=post-modern">post-modern</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48483884-michael-finocchiaro?shelf=american-20th-c">american-20th-c</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/48483884-michael-finocchiaro?shelf=novels">novels</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1699976053" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6290095246598002671">I thoroughly enjoyed Underworld by DeLillo. I was a bit scared of it for years, but after having successfully tackled two other post-modern über-works Infinite Jest and Gravity's Rainbow, I decidedly it was time (admittedly, I have not been able to bring myself to attempt The Recognitions by Gaddis yet). I enjoyed the writing style and loved the story. The background of the postwar 50s and 60s was interesting and I loved the image of the open art exposition in the desert (no spoilers). It was my</span>
  <span id="freeText6290095246598002671" style="display:none">I thoroughly enjoyed Underworld by DeLillo. I was a bit scared of it for years, but after having successfully tackled two other post-modern über-works Infinite Jest and Gravity's Rainbow, I decidedly it was time (admittedly, I have not been able to bring myself to attempt The Recognitions by Gaddis yet). I enjoyed the writing style and loved the story. The background of the postwar 50s and 60s was interesting and I loved the image of the open art exposition in the desert (no spoilers). It was my first book by DeLillo and after now having read 7 others (Players, Falling Man, Libra, White Noise, Mao II, and Zero K), I have to day it is my favorite so far (White Noise and Libra being runner's up and Zero K being my least favorite by far - Ratner's Star is on my shortlist before the end of the year and perhaps I'll try Great Jones Street as well). I thought that the sweeping prose style was more efficient and worked better in this particular story than in the other aforementioned DeLillos. In fact, Underworld may be the only one - besides White Noise- that I will return to in years to come. Honestly, I prefer Pynchon - particularly Mason&amp;Dixon and Against the Day - to DeLillo but of his work, this one was for me the most fun.<br /><br />Since originally writing this review, I have trudged through (and reviewed on GR) The Recognitions and have to say that I preferred Underworld, GR, AtD, M&amp;D, and IJ. Of those four, it would be hard for me to pick a favorite. I think that DeLillo took less chances than Pynchon or DFW, but the narrative is still captivating and entertaining.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6290095246598002671" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_27776753" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/27776753" />
    <a title="Matt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/66632-matt"><img alt="Matt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1178675796p2/66632.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/27776753?book_show_action=true">Jul 20, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt" href="/user/show/66632-matt">Matt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer27776753" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7516412725606132946">The central metaphor in <u>Underworld</u>, as I saw it, revolves around trash. One of the main characters, Nick Shay, works for a waste-disposal company. No matter how many different recycling bins his family divides their waste into (seven and counting), it cannot all be reclaimed. The trash builds up – and what holds true for the physical also holds true for the personal and the historical. No matter how we might try to reprocess, recast,or ignore our history/memory, our past accumulates, and the wei</span>
  <span id="freeText7516412725606132946" style="display:none">The central metaphor in <u>Underworld</u>, as I saw it, revolves around trash. One of the main characters, Nick Shay, works for a waste-disposal company. No matter how many different recycling bins his family divides their waste into (seven and counting), it cannot all be reclaimed. The trash builds up – and what holds true for the physical also holds true for the personal and the historical. No matter how we might try to reprocess, recast,or ignore our history/memory, our past accumulates, and the weight of our mental and personal garbage is heavy.<br /><br />An interesting twist that DeLillo works into <u>Underworld</u>, as I realized during a recent discussion with a friend, is that one of the characters, the painter Klara Sax, is able to find a sort of redemption. Yet the reader sees redemption at the beginning of the book, not the end – the book works backwards towards the trash and detritus of her past, leaving Klara, rather, at an seemingly insurmountable (although we, as readers, know better) low point.<br /><br />One of the greatest successes of the book is the fluidity with which it moves between personal and cultural memories. The opening prologue of the book, in fact, starts off with an incredible recreation of the historic 1951 Dodgers/Giants playoff game – the earliest point, temporarily, in the whole book. When we then jump forward to the present, we meet the characters for the first time – and the rest of the book is spent working backwards, following the personal histories as they weave in and out of the cultural history we met at the beginning. The way in which DeLillo allows these two memories to inform and define each other is an unbelievable triumph, on par with the personal/cultural archives of Joseph Cornell's boxes, from half a century earlier.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7516412725606132946" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="friendReviews elementListBrown" >
    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_150029447" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/150029447" />
    <a title="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5022264-ian-marvin-graye"><img alt="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1594424898p2/5022264.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/150029447?book_show_action=true">Feb 23, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye" href="/user/show/5022264-ian-marvin-graye">Ian &quot;Marvin&quot; Graye</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=reviews">reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=re-read">re-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=read-2020">read-2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=reviews-5-stars">reviews-5-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=exert-yourself">exert-yourself</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5022264-ian-marvin-graye?shelf=de-lillo-rama">de-lillo-rama</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer150029447" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12681014382944629267"><b>CRITIQUE:</b><br/><br/><b>“Underworld" as Found Art/Fiction</b><br/><br/><i>“Underworld"</i> is fascinating in at least two ways: one relates to its use of metafiction, and the other relates to its implicit view of metaphysics:<br/><br/><i>"What did I see in this juxtaposition?"</i><br/><br/><i>"I believed in the stern logic of [connection]."</i><br/><br/><b>“Everything is Connected in the End”</b><br/><br/>A major theme of the novel is that <i>“everything is connected in the end”.</i> DeLillo goes one step beyond theme and uses this proposition as the foundation of the structure of the novel.<br/><br/>Shortl</span>
  <span id="freeText12681014382944629267" style="display:none"><b>CRITIQUE:</b><br><br><b>“Underworld" as Found Art/Fiction</b><br><br><i>“Underworld"</i> is fascinating in at least two ways: one relates to its use of metafiction, and the other relates to its implicit view of metaphysics:<br><br><i>"What did I see in this juxtaposition?"</i><br><br><i>"I believed in the stern logic of [connection]."</i><br><br><b>“Everything is Connected in the End”</b><br><br>A major theme of the novel is that <i>“everything is connected in the end”.</i> DeLillo goes one step beyond theme and uses this proposition as the foundation of the structure of the novel.<br><br>Shortly before he started to write <i>“Underworld”</i> in 1991, DeLillo read a copy of the front page of the New York Times dated October 4, 1951.<br><br>The paper fortuitously provided him with the coupled theme and structure of <i>“Underworld”.</i> He actually describes one of the characters in the novel making the same discovery:<br><blockquote>
<br><i>“The front page astonished him, a pair of three-column headlines dominating. To his left the Giants capture the pennant, beating the Dodgers on a dramatic home run in the ninth inning. And to the right, symmetrically mated, same typeface, same-size type, same number of lines, the USSR explodes an atomic bomb – kaboom – details kept secret.”</i> (668)<br><br><i>“He didn't understand why the Times would take a ball game off the sports page and juxtapose it with news of such ominous consequence.”</i> (668)<br>
</blockquote><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1599940715i/30098141._SX540_.jpg" width="400" height="300" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"> <br><br>The novel uses the same juxtaposition. DeLillo places these two events together <i>(“a coupling all the same...symmetrically mated”),</i> and fills the gaps, or allows the gaps to fill themselves. It’s a masterful experiment/exercise in how juxtaposition encourages content to bleed across and between parts of the framework:<br><br><i>“Find the links. It's all linked.”</i> (577)<br><br>DeLillo lets the novel's plots <i>"dance their way out."</i><br><br><b>The Novel as an Object Passing Through</b><br><br>What links many chapters is the fate of the baseball that was hit into the stands and supposedly found by a young boy called Cotter Martin. In the body of the novel, DeLillo writes:<br><blockquote>
  <br>
  <i>“The ball was an object passing through.”</i>
  <br>
</blockquote><br>The narrative is anything but <i>“the linear arrangement of words on a page".</i> (503) It is a remarkable web, almost like cyberspace, that contains multitudes and lends itself to exploration by HTML.<br><br><b>The Novel as Characters Passing Through</b><br><br>Nick Shay is a principal character, who at some point has bought the baseball <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[for $34,500 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> from a dealer in memorabilia, Marvin Lundy, who has managed to track its lineage/ provenance to the day after the game. What he doesn’t know is that Cotter gave it to his father, Manx Martin, for safekeeping, but his father went out and sold it on the street for approximately $33 to Charles Wainwright. Charles later gives it to his own son, Chuckie, who becomes a navigator of B-52’s that carry atomic bombs. His plane is called Long Tall Sally. <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1599906670i/30096071.jpg" width="400" height="250" alt="description" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>These planes were commonly called BUFF’s (big ugly fat fucks) by the men of the US bomber command. By 1991, most of the planes had been retired to an aircraft boneyard in Arizona, where an artist known as Klara Sax paints them. Klara was formerly married to Albert Bronzini, a science teacher who taught Nick’s brother (Matty) how to play competitive chess. In 1992, Matty is a scientist working at a nuclear weapons facility in New Mexico. Nick had an adulterous sexual relationship with Klara when they both lived in New York in 1951. His own wife, Marian, later has an affair with Nick's work colleague, Brian Glassic. The relationships between women and men are problematic for Nick.<br><br>Young Nick was convicted of the murder of George Manza (the Waiter) with his [George's] own shotgun and sentenced to detention in a correctional facility for juveniles. While Nick feels guilty about George’s death, he’s never been sure whether the death was intentional or accidental, because George had told him that the shotgun was unloaded. Nick works in waste management, and in the prologue flies to Russia and Kazakhstan to witness the test of a waste disposal system that destroys toxic waste in a nuclear explosion. The Russian connection posits a link between weapons and waste. It also marks the end of the Cold War.<br><br><b>Collect/Connect/Correct</b><br><br>As might be apparent from this summary, there is not just a concern with connections. DeLillo structures the novel around the words <i>"collection"</i> <i>(“people collect, collect, always collecting”</i> baseball memorabilia), <i>"connection"</i> and <i>"correction"</i> (of juvenile criminals).<br><br><b>Lucky Strikes</b><br><br>The cigarette brand, Lucky Strikes, also features in the novel. It’s possible that the home runs were the result of a lucky strike, the Soviet nuclear test equally so (from their point of view), and the death of George the Waiter an unlucky strike (from both points of view).<br><br><b>Opposition and Conflict</b><br><br>Just as there are connections between subject matter in the novel, <i>“there’s a lot of opposition and conflict…It's the rule of confrontation...”</i> (27)<br><br>There’s a thin line between rivals, antagonists and enemies. The baseball game was the final between the Giants and the Dodgers on October 3, 1951, while the Soviet nuclear explosion was a defining move in the Cold War. By the end of the prologue, the Cold War appears to have ended in <i>“these wild privatised times…and the marathon of danced-out plots...”</i><br><br>The conflict is an existential threat to everybody present. DeLillo frequently refers to the characters’ being and existence. They participate in episodes, experiences and events, which together constitute history. What remains, lasts or survives is waste, which is <i>“the secret history, the underhistory, the way archaeologists dig out the history of early cultures, every sort of bone heap and broken tool, literally from under the ground.”</i> (791)<br><br><b>Going Underground</b><br><br>Nick’s life has been shaped by his father’s abandonment of his family when Nick was just 11 years of age: <br><blockquote>
<br><i>“The earth opened up and he stepped inside...I think he went under. I don't think he wanted a fresh start or a new life or even an escape. I think he wanted to go under.”</i> (809)<br>
</blockquote><br>In one of several films shown in the novel (including the Zapruder film, Robert Frank's <i>"Cocksucker Blues",</i> and the Texas Highway Killer video), the fictitious <i>“Unterwelt”,</i> <i>“All Eisenstein wants you to see, in the end, are the contradictions of being...You see the inner divisions of people and systems...”</i> (444)<br><br><b>“The Full Weight of Observation”</b><br><br>As he was in his earlier novels, DeLillo remains preoccupied with the mystery of language and the mystery behind and between words:<br><blockquote>
<br><i>“What mattered were the mysteries, not the language in which you said them.”</i> (757)<br>
</blockquote><br>His metaphysics seems to be more based on Jesuit science than post-modern philosophy:<br><blockquote>
<br><i>“We can't see the world clearly until we understand how nature is organised. We need to count, measure and test. This is the scientific method. Science. The observation and description of phenomena. Phenomena. Things perceptible to the senses.”</i> (734)<br><br><i>“How is it that a few marks chalked on a blackboard, a few little squiggly signs can change the shape of human history?...I want to know how it is that a few marks on a slate or a piece of paper, a little black on white, or white on black, can carry so much information and contain such shattering implications. Never mind the energy packed in the atom. What about the energy contained in this equation (E equals MC squared)? This is the real power. How the mind operates. How the mind identifies, analyses and represents. What beauty and power. What marvels of imagination does it require to reduce the complex forces of nature, all those unseeable magical actions inside the atom – to express all this with a bing and a bang on a blackboard.”</i> (736)<br>
</blockquote><br><b>The Jesuitical Novel</b><br><br>Nick makes the Jesuitical influence even more explicit: <br><blockquote>
<br><i>“The Jesuits taught me to examine things for second meanings and deeper connections.”</i> (88)<br>
</blockquote><br>In the end, DeLillo's quest for meaning and connection becomes a plea for the cessation of hostilities between nations and ideologies, and a declaration of peace in the battle of the sexes.<br><br><br><br><b>FOUND VERSE:<br>[In the Words of Don DeLillo]</b><br><br><b>Sister Edgar Reads Poe at School<br>[Mostly In the Words of Don DeLillo]</b><br><br>She paced the floor and would become <br>The poem and the raven both, <br>A fearless bird, roman-nosed, <br>Gliding out of the timeless sky,<br>Defining its own airy path <br>And diving down upon the class.<br><br><br><b>The Telemetry of Crowds</b><br><br>An understanding <br>Seemed to travel <br>Through the audience, <br>Conveyed row by row <br>In that mysterious <br>Telemetry of crowds. <br>Or maybe not so mysterious. <br><br><br><b>Tangerine Dream</b><br><br>Language is webbed<br>In the senses,<br>Out of sand-blaze<br>Brilliance into<br>Touch, taste and <br>Fragrance. <br><br><br><b>The Safe Place of Imagination</b><br><br>Those bedridden days<br>Of childhood<br>When he was islanded<br>In sheets and pillows,<br>Surrounded by books<br>And chess pieces,<br>Deliciously sick at times,<br>A fever that sent him inward,<br>Sea-sweats and dreams<br>With runny colours,<br>Lonely but not unhappy,<br>The room a world,<br>The safe place of imagination.<br><br><br><b>Longing for Disarray</b><br><br>I long for the days of disarray, <br>When I didn't give a damn <br>Or a fuck or a farthing. <br><br><br><b>Secretion</b><br><br>What we excrete<br>Comes back<br>To consume us.<br><br><br><b>From the Beach to the Bronx</b><br><br>They had the rear seat<br>To themselves<br>On the ride back,<br>The motor right below them,<br>Heat beating up,<br>And they dozed<br>On each other's shoulders,<br>Faces sun-tight   <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/176373629?book_show_action=true">Jun 13, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Adam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Adam" href="/user/show/1884982-adam">Adam</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780684848150" href="/book/show/399.Underworld">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1884982-adam?shelf=1970-present">1970-present</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1884982-adam?shelf=prose">prose</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer176373629" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8747117080407740426">This is now my favourite novel alongside <i>Blood Meridian</i>, <i>2666</i> and <i>Infinite Jest</i>. I'm too fatigued and mentally exhausted to write a decent review now, which fact is a shame. <br/><br/><i>Underworld</i> is, to use a quote from Roberto Bolaño's <i>2666</i> to illustrate my take on this DeLillo novel, one of "the great, imperfect, torrential works, books that blaze a path into the unknown."<br/><br/>Those who will tell you that <i>White Noise</i> is DeLillo's best, or some other short, compact, precise DeLillo work, "want to watch the gre</span>
  <span id="freeText8747117080407740426" style="display:none">This is now my favourite novel alongside <i>Blood Meridian</i>, <i>2666</i> and <i>Infinite Jest</i>. I'm too fatigued and mentally exhausted to write a decent review now, which fact is a shame. <br /><br /><i>Underworld</i> is, to use a quote from Roberto Bolaño's <i>2666</i> to illustrate my take on this DeLillo novel, one of "the great, imperfect, torrential works, books that blaze a path into the unknown."<br /><br />Those who will tell you that <i>White Noise</i> is DeLillo's best, or some other short, compact, precise DeLillo work, "want to watch the great masters spar, but they have no interest in real combat, when the great masters struggle against that something, that something that terrifies us all, that something that cows us and spurs us on, amid blood and mortal wounds and stench." THAT is what DeLillo does here. <br /><br /><i>Underworld</i> is DeLillo's <i>The Trial</i>, his <i>Moby Dick</i>, his <i>Bouvard and Pecuchet</i>. It is not his <i>Metamorphosis</i>, his <i>Bartleby</i>, his <i>A Simple Heart</i>. <br /><br />Like all great writers, DeLillo's given you the chance to watch him spar if that's what you want, and there's nothing necessarily wrong with that. But as far as I'm concerned, nothing is as beautiful as reading a book by a literary master embroiled in what Bolaño terms "real combat" and so eloquently describes in the quote above. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8747117080407740426" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2577259" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2577259" />
    <a title="Joseph" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/164002-joseph"><img alt="Joseph" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1220553451p2/164002.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2577259?book_show_action=true">Jun 30, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joseph" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joseph" href="/user/show/164002-joseph">Joseph</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780684848150" href="/book/show/399.Underworld">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/164002-joseph?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2577259" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17054833412245974898">An excellent example of the critical consensus being just plain wrong. Underworld is bloated, confused, and turgid - yet critics who should have known better drowned it in praise. I think this is due to a number of factors. <br/><br/>One, pedigree: DeLillo is a critical darling, deservedly so. Two, Heft: just like in movies, critics assume size equals importance, and thus the longer it takes to get through something, the more that something must have to say. It's 854 pages, 600 of which could have been cu</span>
  <span id="freeText17054833412245974898" style="display:none">An excellent example of the critical consensus being just plain wrong. Underworld is bloated, confused, and turgid - yet critics who should have known better drowned it in praise. I think this is due to a number of factors. <br /><br />One, pedigree: DeLillo is a critical darling, deservedly so. Two, Heft: just like in movies, critics assume size equals importance, and thus the longer it takes to get through something, the more that something must have to say. It's 854 pages, 600 of which could have been cut. Three, it's Delillo, who rivals <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6149.Beloved" title="Beloved by Toni Morrison" rel="nofollow noopener">Toni Morrison</a> and John Updike for riding the line between brilliant and laughably overwrought and critics will always prefer the "difficult" to the plainspoken. Fine by me - I don't have a problem with occasionally making the reader work for his/her supper. But there's a difference between challenging the reader and flexing your cleverness, and you can guess which one I think DeLillo does here. Ultimately, I don't think DeLillo knew what his story was about and tried to compenstate by adding more and more pages. Critics, never wanting to be the one who doesn't "get it", fawned and fellated the book, doing no favors to either the author or readers who mistakenly wade into this dank swamp and wonder why they're so dumb for not seeing the brilliance. And then they run back to James Patterson or Nicholas Sparks or some shit like that and we're all a little poorer in the end. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17054833412245974898" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_849746847" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/849746847" />
    <a title="Sentimental Surrealist" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9707921-sentimental-surrealist"><img alt="Sentimental Surrealist" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1412974239p2/9707921.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/849746847?book_show_action=true">Feb 09, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sentimental Surrealist" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sentimental Surrealist" href="/user/show/9707921-sentimental-surrealist">Sentimental Surrealist</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9707921-sentimental-surrealist?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9707921-sentimental-surrealist?shelf=postmodernism">postmodernism</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9707921-sentimental-surrealist?shelf=don-delillo">don-delillo</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9707921-sentimental-surrealist?shelf=doorstopper">doorstopper</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9707921-sentimental-surrealist?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9707921-sentimental-surrealist?shelf=someone-here-is-lying">someone-here-is-lying</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9707921-sentimental-surrealist?shelf=1001-books">1001-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9707921-sentimental-surrealist?shelf=collection">collection</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer849746847" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3180223232890150454">With every DeLillo novel I read, I realize that Underworld is the pinnacle of the man's artistry. Every novel he wrote beforehand leads up to it, hints at it, contains thematic foreshadowings of it, and the sixty-odd pages of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28703.Cosmopolis' title='Cosmopolis by Don DeLillo' rel='nofollow noopener'>Cosmopolis</a> I've read are so far from this that it seems DeLillo understood there was no going back to his older style, because he'd already perfected it. This, of course, invites the possibility that DeLillo could release another masterpiece in his later style, but with the</span>
  <span id="freeText3180223232890150454" style="display:none">With every DeLillo novel I read, I realize that Underworld is the pinnacle of the man's artistry. Every novel he wrote beforehand leads up to it, hints at it, contains thematic foreshadowings of it, and the sixty-odd pages of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/28703.Cosmopolis" title="Cosmopolis by Don DeLillo" rel="nofollow noopener">Cosmopolis</a> I've read are so far from this that it seems DeLillo understood there was no going back to his older style, because he'd already perfected it. This, of course, invites the possibility that DeLillo could release another masterpiece in his later style, but with the man getting up there age-wise and with Underworld having twenty-plus years of DeLillo Mark I (or DeLillo Marks I and II, depending on how you define terms) to draw on, it's hard for me to see him ever topping this. <br /><br />Like most of the major postmodern novels, Underworld is a beast of a book. It's long, it's dense, its character roster is massive, and it runs through the twentieth century's eventful second half with grace, insight, and humor. Real-life figures such as J. Edgar Hoover, Lenny Bruce, and Sergei Eisenstein all factor in, with Bruce brought to vivid and hilarious life throughout several chapters towards the novel's end. It begins with a breathless retelling of Bobby Thompson's shot heard 'round the world juxtaposed against another such shot, the Russian testing of a nuclear warhead, and ends with an equally breathless tale of poverty, terrorism and the internet. If you've only experienced DeLillo through the artful awkwardness of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11762.White_Noise" title="White Noise by Don DeLillo" rel="nofollow noopener">White Noise</a>, you'll see an entirely different side of the author here.<br /><br />Of course, it couldn't have happened without the novels that came before. DeLillo writes about poverty, consumerism, mass media, fear of death, conspiracies, strained sexual relationships, crowd psychology, history, language, and everything else he'd done before, but here he weaves it all together into a dense and beautiful tapestry. The use and weaving of these diverse themes, coupled with the novel's unique structure - it starts in the '50s, shoots forward into the '90s, digs decade by decade back into the '50s, and then leaps forty years into the future for its grand conclusion, thus creating an excavation for history - make it a novel like none other before it. This means that, while it occasionally overreaches, the overreaching can be forgiven: since DeLillo's working without a net, it's inevitable and acceptable that he sometimes runs ahead of himself. Besides, who wants to read authors that sit around in their own little bubbles all day?<br /><br />Underworld is a big, bold, astonishing and challenging work, one that asks a lot from you and rewards the patient reader. In love with history and language, it's a novel of ideas that tells a hell of a story, a story much broader than you might expect it to be. Definitely one of the preeminent works of twentieth century fiction.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3180223232890150454" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/271815205" />
    <a title="Nathan &quot;N.R.&quot; Gaddis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7607437-nathan-n-r-gaddis"><img alt="Nathan &quot;N.R.&quot; Gaddis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548295774p2/7607437.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/271815205?book_show_action=true">Feb 03, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nathan &quot;N.R.&quot; Gaddis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nathan &quot;N.R.&quot; Gaddis" href="/user/show/7607437-nathan-n-r-gaddis">Nathan &quot;N.R.&quot; Gaddis</a>

          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780684842691" href="/book/show/4266011-underworld">review of another edition</a>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7607437-nathan-n-r-gaddis?shelf=too-big-to-knot">too-big-to-knot</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7607437-nathan-n-r-gaddis?shelf=encyclopedic">encyclopedic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7607437-nathan-n-r-gaddis?shelf=2014-gelesen">2014-gelesen</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7607437-nathan-n-r-gaddis?shelf=100-mccaffery-read">100-mccaffery-read</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer271815205" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer15604543076118936129">There is no review here. I’m merely registering a score.<br/><br/>It’s been years since I last picked up a DeLillo. This one’s been waiting far too long. I rewatched the Cosmopolis movie last week. It’s not good. <i>Underworld</i> is pretty fine.<br/><br/>My motivation was this. There are Underworld detractors on gr who I’m almost certain should have known better. There are Underworld boosters on gr who have never (not quite) convinced me. So I set out to do that thing which I rarely need to bother doing myself, Making up</span>
  <span id="freeText15604543076118936129" style="display:none">There is no review here. I’m merely registering a score.<br /><br />It’s been years since I last picked up a DeLillo. This one’s been waiting far too long. I rewatched the Cosmopolis movie last week. It’s not good. <i>Underworld</i> is pretty fine.<br /><br />My motivation was this. There are Underworld detractors on gr who I’m almost certain should have known better. There are Underworld boosters on gr who have never (not quite) convinced me. So I set out to do that thing which I rarely need to bother doing myself, Making up my own mind about a gargantuan postmodern tome. I had already four DeLillos tucked away, but hadn’t quite decided about him. Underworld would be a break it or make it? Maybe.<br /><br />Results. The detractors should know better. This is a pretty great novel. The boosters I think oversell a bit. I’ll be reading more DeLillo.<br /><br />Here’s the thing. During most of my reading of Underworld I felt it pulled in two directions, in the directions of two other novels; <i>Women and Men</i> and <i>Infinite Jest</i>. Clearly for different reasons. And I thought both of those novels did what they did better than how Underworld did it. In part because Underworld gave me that rare experience of feeling a bulking novel was slightly too baggy. I mean maybe fifty pages too baggy ; but mostly the bagginess would have been fixed probably by adding <i>more</i> pages. No need to take stuff out. But sometimes, and perhaps here in Underworld, that pace is important and “tightening it up” would have destroyed the rhythm which comes with a certain spaciousness. At any rate, I got the century-long expansiveness in a richer measure with Women and Men and I got the pyrotechnic/smart prose better in Infinite Jest. Not to mind. <br /><br />But one thing’s for sure ; stop boosting that short story that begins the novel. It’s an overture. You know The Lone Ranger, but do you know William Tell? “Das Kapital”, the epilogue, is also quite fine. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15604543076118936129" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>
